<div
class="right_col
col-a-2">













<div
class="header
artist_info_header
int-1
clearfix">














<h1
class="hed-1"
title="Death
Cab
For
Cutie"
itemprop="name">Death
Cab
For
Cutie</h1>














<div
class="header_btns">















<div
class="bookmark"
data-musicid="R830"></div>














</div>













</div>













<div
class="int-1">














<div
class="content_right">















<div
class="artist_bio_container
clearfix">
















<div
class="artist_bio
short_bio">
















<div
class="artist_bio_inner">

















Death
Cab
for
Cutie's
rise
from
small-time
solo
project
to
Grammy-nominated
rock
band
is
one
of
indie
rock's
greatest
success
stories.
Launched
in
the
bayside
college
town
of
Bellingham,
Washington,
the
group
was
originally
a
side
project
for
singer/guitarist


















<a
href="/benjamin-gibbard"
class="hash">Ben
Gibbard</a>,
an
engineering
student
at
Western
Washington
University
who
split
his
time
between
school
and
music.
Taking
a
break
from
his
local
power
pop
band,
Pinwheel,


















<a
href="/benjamin-gibbard"
class="hash">Gibbard</a>
began
recording
an
album's
worth
of
solo
material
during
the
summer
of
1997.
Producer


















<a
href="/chris-walla"
class="hash">Chris
Walla</a>
lent
his
help
to
the
sessions,
which
resulted
in
an
eight-song
cassette
entitled


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/you-can-play-these-songs-with-chords"
class="hash">You
Can
Play
These
Songs
with
Chords</a>.
When
the
tape
became
a
local
hit,


















<a
href="/benjamin-gibbard"
class="hash">Gibbard</a>
reached
into
his
circle
of
friends
to
form
a
band,
hoping
to
play
the
new
songs
live.
Bassist
Nick
Harmer
(

















<a
href="/benjamin-gibbard"
class="hash">Gibbard</a>'s
roommate)
and
drummer
Nathan
Good
climbed
aboard,
and


















<a
href="/chris-walla"
class="hash">Walla</a>
enlisted
as
the
band's
primary
guitarist
(he
would
also
go
on
to
produce
most
of
the
band's
future
releases).
With
a
lineup
now
in
place,


















<a
href="/benjamin-gibbard"
class="hash">Gibbard</a>'s
group
rechristened
itself
Death
Cab
for
Cutie
(named
after
a
song
by


















<a
href="/bonzo-dog-band"
class="hash">the
Bonzo
Dog
Doo
Dah
Band</a>)
and
signed
a
contract
with
the
Seattle-based
Barsuk
Records
within
a
year's
time.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
The
quartet
made
its
studio
debut
with
1998's


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/something-about-airplanes"
class="hash">Something
About
Airplanes</a>,
an
album
that
featured
several
re-recorded
tracks
from
the


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/you-can-play-these-songs-with-chords"
class="hash">You
Can
Play
These
Songs
with
Chords</a>
cassette
as
well
as
a
dreamy,
pop-oriented
sound
reminiscent
of


















<a
href="/built-to-spill-caustic-resin"
class="hash">Built
to
Spill</a>.


















<a
href="/benjamin-gibbard"
class="hash">Gibbard</a>
and


















<a
href="/chris-walla"
class="hash">Walla</a>
both
continued
to
pursue
their
own
projects
(including


















<a
href="/benjamin-gibbard"
class="hash">Gibbard</a>'s
successful
stint
with


















<a
href="/postal-service"
class="hash">the
Postal
Service</a>),
but
that
didn't
keep
Death
Cab
for
Cutie
from
returning
to
the
studio
for
a
second
album,


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/we-have-facts-were-voting-yes"
class="hash">We
Have
the
Facts
and
We're
Voting
Yes</a>,
which
appeared
in
2000.
Nathan
Good
left
the
group
just
prior
to
the
album's
completion,
and


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/we-have-facts-were-voting-yes"
class="hash">We
Have
the
Facts</a>
introduced
Michael
Schorr
as
Death
Cab's
new
drummer.
The


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/forbidden-love-ep"
class="hash">Forbidden
Love</a>
EP
arrived
that
same
year,
while
a
third
full-length
effort,


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/photo-album"
class="hash">The
Photo
Album</a>,
was
released
in
2001.
By
this
time,
a
sizable
audience
had
gathered
around
the
band's
emotional
music,
and
Barsuk
re-released


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/you-can-play-these-songs-with-chords"
class="hash">You
Can
Play
These
Songs
with
Chords</a>
in
2002
with
ten
additional
songs.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
The
polished,
hook-laden
Transatlanticism
arrived
in
2003
and
announced
the
arrival
of
drummer
Jason
McGerr,
who
had
previously
played
in
a
band
with
Nick
Harmer
before
Death
Cab's
formation.
The
album
also
proved
to
be
a
very
important
step
in
the
band's
career,
gathering
positive
attention
from
consumers
and
industry
execs
(including
television
producer
Josh
Schwartz,
who
prominently
featured
the
band's
music
throughout
several
seasons
of


















<i>The
O.C.</i>).
With
their
popularity
at
an
all-time
high,
the
bandmates
issued
a
live
disc,


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/john-byrd-ep-live"
class="hash">The
John
Byrd
E.P.</a>,
and
later
signed
a
worldwide
major-label
deal
with
Atlantic
Records
in
November
2004.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/plans"
class="hash">Plans</a>
was
released
the
following
summer
and
debuted
at
number
four,
remaining
on
the
Billboard
charts
for
nearly
one
year
and
achieving
platinum
status
on
the
strength
of
three
singles
(including
the
acoustic
ballad
&quot;I
Will
Follow
You
into
the
Dark&quot;).
Death
Cab
for
Cutie
graced
the
cover
of
Spin
magazine,
appeared
on
an
episode
of


















<i>Saturday
Night
Live</i>,
and
earned
a
Grammy
nomination
for
their
major-label
debut.
Work
on
a
follow-up
album
coincided
with
the
release
of


















<a
href="/chris-walla"
class="hash">Chris
Walla</a>'s
solo
effort,


















<a
href="/chris-walla/field-manual"
class="hash">Field
Manual</a>,
and
Death
Cab
returned
in
May
2008
with


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/narrow-stairs"
class="hash">Narrow
Stairs</a>,
a
darker
effort
that
debuted
at
the
top
of
the
Billboard
200.
The
band
proceeded
to
tour
throughout
the
remainder
of
the
year,
while
a
deluxe
version
of


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/something-about-airplanes"
class="hash">Something
About
Airplanes</a>
(which
was
packaged
with
a
recording
of
their
very
first
show
in
Seattle)
was
released
in
November
to
introduce
newer
fans
to
Death
Cab's
early
material.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
Death
Cab
for
Cutie
continued
touring
throughout
the
first
half
of
2009,
hitting
Japan
and
Australia
as
well
as
an
additional
slew
of
American
venues.


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/open-door-ep"
class="hash">The
Open
Door
EP</a>
arrived
that
spring,
featuring
several
scrapped
songs
from
the


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/narrow-stairs"
class="hash">Narrow
Stairs</a>
sessions
and
a
demo
version
of
&quot;Talking
Bird.&quot;
The
guys
incorporated
some
of
those
songs
into
their
live
sets,
all
the
while
preparing
to
return
to
the
studio
after
the
tour's
completion.
After
a
short
hiatus
--
not
to
mention
a
wedding
for


















<a
href="/benjamin-gibbard"
class="hash">Ben
Gibbard</a>,
who
married
actress/musician
Zooey
Deschanel
--
they
reconvened
for
2011's


















<a
href="/death-cab-for-cutie/codes-keys"
class="hash">Codes
and
Keys</a>,
which
found
the
band
relying
less
on
the
electric
guitar
and
more
on
moody,


















<a
href="/cure"
class="hash">Cure</a>-inspired
song
textures.
Later
in
2011
(right
around
the
same
time


















<a
href="/benjamin-gibbard"
class="hash">Gibbard</a>
and
Deschanel
announced
their
split)
they
released
an
EP
of
remixes
of
songs
from
the
album
titled
Keys
and
Codes
Remix
EP.
~
Andrew
Leahey,
Rovi
















</div>















</div>
















<a
href="#"
class="full_bio_link">full
bio</a>















</div>
